    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8025014479486806859">Berger, son of the failed poet L.B. Berger, is in the grips of an obsession. The Gursky family with its colourful bootlegging history, its bizarre connections with the North and the Inuit, and its wildly eccentric relations, both fascinates and infuriates him. His quest to unravel their story leads to the enigmatic Ephraim Gursky: document forger in Victorian England, sole</span>
  <span id="freeText8025014479486806859" style="display:none">Berger, son of the failed poet L.B. Berger, is in the grips of an obsession. The Gursky family with its colourful bootlegging history, its bizarre connections with the North and the Inuit, and its wildly eccentric relations, both fascinates and infuriates him. His quest to unravel their story leads to the enigmatic Ephraim Gursky: document forger in Victorian England, sole survivor of the ill-fated Franklin expedition and charasmatic religious leader of the Arctic. Of Ephraim's three grandsons, Bernard has fought, wheeled and cheated his way to the head of a liquor empire. His brother Morrie has reluctantly followed along. But how does Ephraim's protege, Solomon, fit in? Elusive, mysterious and powerful, Solomon Gursky hovers in the background, always out of Moses' grasp, but present-like an omen.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8025014479486806859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

While I can understand the resentment that Morrie had for his brother, unless I missed something, I don't quite understand what he would have against his secretary.<br /><br />Anyone have any thoughts? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/9759317-deadweight"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1450211841i/9759317._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/9759317-deadweight">DeadWeight</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    O'Brien is just the means to some ends - Morrie, who as it turns out is as cuthroat and single-minded as his brothers were, knows that O'Brien knows t<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    O'Brien is just the means to some ends - Morrie, who as it turns out is as cuthroat and single-minded as his brothers were, knows that O'Brien knows things which could take down Bernard's legacy and his children with it. Morrie knows the same things, but can't leak them himself. Coming from O'Brien, however, Bernard's secretary and mistress, who will now, Morrie knows, go running to whoever will listen (and ends up with Moses), is going to spill the beans and doom Lionel's hold over the company.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_1991672237" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1991672237" />
    <a title="Hugh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32318315-hugh"><img alt="Hugh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466451007p2/32318315.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1991672237?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hugh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hugh" href="/user/show/32318315-hugh">Hugh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099877301" href="/book/show/862408.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=read-2017">read-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=booker-shortlist">booker-shortlist</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1991672237" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15506546716011467374">This is an ambitious, confusing and sometimes crazy mixture of fact and fantasy. It tells the story of an ultimately rich Jewish Canadian family from the early nineteenth century to the 1980s. The story is loosely held together by Moses Berger, an alcoholic writer obsessed with the family who has accumulated scraps of information over a lifetime. <br/><br/>At the heart of the story are the legends of the family's founding father Ephraim, a small-time criminal in London who somehow inveigles himself a plac</span>
  <span id="freeText15506546716011467374" style="display:none">This is an ambitious, confusing and sometimes crazy mixture of fact and fantasy. It tells the story of an ultimately rich Jewish Canadian family from the early nineteenth century to the 1980s. The story is loosely held together by Moses Berger, an alcoholic writer obsessed with the family who has accumulated scraps of information over a lifetime. <br /><br />At the heart of the story are the legends of the family's founding father Ephraim, a small-time criminal in London who somehow inveigles himself a place on Franklin's ill-fated expedition to find the North-West passage. In Richler's version of the story, Ephraim is the only survivor, first through cannibalism and then by persuading the local Inuit to follow his religious cult. <br /><br />Ephraim's three grandsons are Bernard, Solomon and Morrie, who we first meet in a remote hotel in rural Saskatchewan where their father is a horse trader. Solomon bets their entire future in a poker game and wins the hotel, and the money that enables them to start a business, initially as bootleggers but eventually as a respectable business, which is run by the controlling patriarch Bernard after the charismatic Solomon disappears in a mysterious plane crash. <br /><br />These are just two of the many strands of a tale that encompasses many disparate elements, and allows Richler to indulge his interests in history, Inuit customs, Judaism and much else besides. <br /><br />The book is deliberately muddled, partly to reflect Moses's addled mind, and partly to allow some surprising revelations to be held back until quite late. For me it is too long, and I did feel that the female characters' roles were very limited, but the best parts are very good indeed. <br /><br />I read it as part of <b>The Mookse and the Gripes</b> group's project to analyse the 1990 Booker prize shortlist. 1990 was another very strong year, and I can't place this one any higher that fifth, but in other years it might have been a strong contender, and I would be interested in reading more Richler.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15506546716011467374" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_61145960" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61145960" />
    <a title="K" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13779-k"><img alt="K" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61145960?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K" href="/user/show/13779-k">K</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">die-hard Richler fans who like Dickensian-type sagas</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13779-k?shelf=humorous-or-trying-to-be">humorous-or-trying-to-be</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13779-k?shelf=jewish">jewish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61145960" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9246079844574857377">Imagine if I told you the story of Dr. Seuss’s “The Cat in the Hat” in the following way:<br/><br/>Chapter 1 – the birth of Thing One and Thing Two<br/>Chapter 2 – after the children’s mother comes home at the end<br/>Chapter 3 – the cat’s early childhood years<br/>Chapter 4 – the fish’s perspective as the cat wrecks the house<br/><br/>And so on, and so forth, for 400 pages. Reading this book was a similar experience.<br/><br/>The basic plot of “Solomon Gursky was Here” focuses on the rise and exploits of the notoriously wealthy and powerf</span>
  <span id="freeText9246079844574857377" style="display:none">Imagine if I told you the story of Dr. Seuss’s “The Cat in the Hat” in the following way:<br /><br />Chapter 1 – the birth of Thing One and Thing Two<br />Chapter 2 – after the children’s mother comes home at the end<br />Chapter 3 – the cat’s early childhood years<br />Chapter 4 – the fish’s perspective as the cat wrecks the house<br /><br />And so on, and so forth, for 400 pages. Reading this book was a similar experience.<br /><br />The basic plot of “Solomon Gursky was Here” focuses on the rise and exploits of the notoriously wealthy and powerful (and of course, highly dysfunctional) Gursky family, and on the self-destructive alcoholic would-be scholar, Moses Berger, who has become obsessed with documenting their story. Moses is particularly fixated on Solomon, the middle brother, whose alleged death is shrouded in mystery and may have been caused by Solomon’s older brother Bernard.<br /><br />Several reviewers described this ambitious book as “Dickensian” and I agree – the basic story becomes a far-reaching saga with tangential episodes focusing on all sorts of peripheral characters. The problem is, I never much liked Dickens. In my opinion, Dickens’s novels reflected the fact that they had started out as magazine serials where he was paid by the word, and that he profited by drawing out the story as long as he possibly could. Writing long, sprawling epics rather than tight, focused stories may have worked for Dickens financially, but it doesn’t work artistically, at least for me. <br /><br />I really appreciate Mordecai Richler as a writer, and it kills me to give him just two stars. For me, though, this was not one of his better books although it was certainly more ambitious and far-reaching than his others. The story was simply too long, dense, and convoluted for me. Richler constantly went back and forth between a multitude of characters (often peripheral to the story) and time periods, and it was difficult to keep everything straight. There were constant vague references to climactic events which I only started to get 2-300 pages later, when I no longer remembered the references or how they came in. I started to get into the story about halfway through, which is a little late for a 400+ page book. Even once I got into it, I never found the book particularly compelling.<br /><br />As an aside, I suspect this book had a strong role in inspiring Michael Chabon’s “The Yiddish Policemen’s Union.” I had a strong sense of déjà vu as I read about tough Arctic Jews consorting with Eskimos. I give Richler credit, though, for knowing his Yiddish and his Jewish rituals and lifestyle way better than Chabon did. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9246079844574857377" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_124825083" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124825083" />
    <a title="·Karen·" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652898-karen"><img alt="·Karen·" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434742215p2/1652898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124825083?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="·Karen·" class="user" itemprop="url" name="·Karen·" href="/user/show/1652898-karen">·Karen·</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099877301" href="/book/show/862408.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=canada">canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124825083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10306746127630339651">What a rollicking humdinger of a roller coaster ride! Moses Berger's obsession with the Gursky dynasty pitches the reader from an early 19th century Durham coal mine to the 1980s Eastern Townships, with Franklin's disastrous voyage to find the Northwest passage and the building of a commercial empire based on the sale and production of alcohol with the murkiest, muddiest, most questionable of methods that slide in and out of legality in a slippery and deadly game. Five generations of Gurskys, in</span>
  <span id="freeText10306746127630339651" style="display:none">What a rollicking humdinger of a roller coaster ride! Moses Berger's obsession with the Gursky dynasty pitches the reader from an early 19th century Durham coal mine to the 1980s Eastern Townships, with Franklin's disastrous voyage to find the Northwest passage and the building of a commercial empire based on the sale and production of alcohol with the murkiest, muddiest, most questionable of methods that slide in and out of legality in a slippery and deadly game. Five generations of Gurskys, interspersed with Berger's own growing realisation that he was never the fisherman, but always the fish, caught and played and made to dance across the water by Solomon, the central figure, whose mysterious death provides the narrative drive. The story is not told in a linear way, but bucks and switches between time and place, from Canada to China and all points between, mixing legend and truth, mixing Jewish and Inuit mythology. A certain amount of tenacity is a prerequisite for the reader; it took me until part five, a good 300 pages in, before the characters began to come together and hold a place in my mind, before I began to see a pattern emerging. It is not so much the unravelling of a knot, it is more a whole skein of loose threads that are, at last, knitted up into a complete picture. <br /><br />Small note on the edition: I wouldn't recommend this Vintage paperback, it was proof read by a dyslexic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10306746127630339651" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1653449011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10016117216755296155">this was a re-read for me... but i last read it when it was published (1989) and have a crap memory. so all i retained was the barest of strings, and the sense of just loving this story.<br/><br/>i have to say that i get so much enjoyment out of reading richler (and, as with carol shields, i get bummed fairly frequently over the fact they are no longer here to share new work with us). if the word 'romp' were ever well used in reviewing a book, it would be for this novel. it's a total romp. (can't believe </span>
  <span id="freeText10016117216755296155" style="display:none">this was a re-read for me... but i last read it when it was published (1989) and have a crap memory. so all i retained was the barest of strings, and the sense of just loving this story.<br /><br />i have to say that i get so much enjoyment out of reading richler (and, as with carol shields, i get bummed fairly frequently over the fact they are no longer here to share new work with us). if the word 'romp' were ever well used in reviewing a book, it would be for this novel. it's a total romp. (can't believe i'm using that word!) it's epic and grand, fun and sharp, and for all its literariness, there is also an interesting mystery.<br /><br />in her review for the NY Times, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12180.Francine_Prose" title="Francine Prose" rel="nofollow noopener">Francine Prose</a> says this of the book: <blockquote>
  <i>"In this, his ninth and most complex novel, Mr. Richler, a Canadian, is after something ambitious and risky, something slightly Dickensian, magical realist: ''Two Hundred Years of Jewish-Canadian Solitude.'' Richler fans will find the scenes one expects in his work -funny, biting, snide-sympathetic takes on Montreal Jewish life - incorporated into a fanciful superstructure of history, geography, myth... Regardless of what its author may actually have experienced, ''Solomon Gursky Was Here'' reads as if it were great fun to write. Dense, intricately plotted, it takes exuberant, nose-thumbing joy in traditional storytelling with all its nervy cliffhangers and narrative hooks, its windfall legacies, stolen portraits, murders and revenges, its clues that drop on the story line with a satisfying thud."<i></i></i>
</blockquote> and i think the cool thing prose hit on in her review was the aspect of fun -- as i was reading i kept hoping richer had as much fun writing this as i was having reading it. there seems to be a whole lot of mischievous joy seeping from the pages, and that was a great experience!<br /><br />(here's the link to prose's review, if you are interested, written 08 april 1990: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.nytimes.com/books/97/12/21/home/richler-gursky.html">https://www.nytimes.com/books/97/12/2...</a> )</span>

<div id="review_1990622258" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1990622258" />
    <a title="Jonathan Pool" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27950748-jonathan-pool"><img alt="Jonathan Pool" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484567350p2/27950748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1990622258?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Pool" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Pool" href="/user/show/27950748-jonathan-pool">Jonathan Pool</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394539959" href="/book/show/1775541.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27950748-jonathan-pool?shelf=booker-to-2015">booker-to-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27950748-jonathan-pool?shelf=international">international</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27950748-jonathan-pool?shelf=booker-listed">booker-listed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1990622258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13599110695917688204">My second Booker 1990 shortlist read as part of the Mookse and Gripes group revisit.<br/><br/>This is a novel that asks a lot of its reader. At times it's tempting to quit, such is the seemingly random sequence of events and characters thrown into the mix. Timelines are not only non linear; there seems to be little reason why one passage precedes and follows another.<br/>While there are nominally eight sections, each with roughly seven chapters, it would not change the reading experience if each of the (roughl</span>
  <span id="freeText13599110695917688204" style="display:none">My second Booker 1990 shortlist read as part of the Mookse and Gripes group revisit.<br /><br />This is a novel that asks a lot of its reader. At times it's tempting to quit, such is the seemingly random sequence of events and characters thrown into the mix. Timelines are not only non linear; there seems to be little reason why one passage precedes and follows another.<br />While there are nominally eight sections, each with roughly seven chapters, it would not change the reading experience if each of the (roughly) fifty six chapters were cast into the air, re-assembled and then read in the new, randomly formed sequence.<br />Solomon Gursky is a chaotic read. The question is whether Richler pulls it off in, obviously deliberately, writing a story that constantly shifts focal points. Minor characters, newly introduced characters, spring out of the page, and the reader is rarely allowed to get comfortable.<br /><br />There are compensations.<br />Richler writes great comedy.<br />L.B Berger says of his son:<br /><i>"If he is really determined to become a writer he is certain to be compared to me and suffer for it. Possibly I never should have had a child. It was indulgent of me"</i>(96)<br />(L.B. isn't particularly talented, as it happens!!).<br /><br />Many of the individual characters are compelling, in different ways:<br />L.B Berger, for his arrogance<br />Mr. Bernard Gursky, for his frustrations and paranoia.<br />Solomon Gursky; for his quick and witty retort to any provocation, and his raw ability to excel at everything.<br />Henry and Isaac for their willfulness.<br />Ephraim Gursky as the great pioneer and philanderer.<br />Then there are the other hundred characters who emerge at society parties; who hang out at bars; the many advisors to the Gursky business empire; mysterious lovers and muses.<br /><br />Solomon Gursky Was Here charges through Canadian history in the Prohibition era. It is a worthy Booker short list candidate in my opinion. I suspect that it will annoy, baffle and alienate readers while, in equal measure, it wins new admirers. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13599110695917688204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7793764630282779334">What a mess! ... This was my first and likely last Richler novel. I was expecting something a lot more coherent out of a man who was supposedly one of the great Canadian novelists. This is a ridiculously overcooked, meandering book with little-to-no focus. I think as late as the final few chapters, Richler was STILL introducing new characters!! I find it pretty infuriating when an author does that. <br/><br/>The book is far too grandiose in scope.   <span id="freeText7793764630282779334" style="display:none">What a mess! ... This was my first and likely last Richler novel. I was expecting something a lot more coherent out of a man who was supposedly one of the great Canadian novelists. This is a ridiculously overcooked, meandering book with little-to-no focus. I think as late as the final few chapters, Richler was STILL introducing new characters!! I find it pretty infuriating when an author does that. <br /><br />The book is far too grandiose in scope. He introduces too many characters, too many disconnected scenes, and the overall focus of the story becomes clouded long before the end.<br /><br />Maybe his earlier books, which seemed somewhat simpler in scope e.g. Duddy Kravitz, were more coherent. This, however, is an incoherent mess and it's absolutely laughable to me that it was nominated for the Booker Prize. Overlong, convoluted, a true mess of a book.</span>

        <a title="Mark Lisac" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Lisac" href="/user/show/1554782-mark-lisac">Mark Lisac</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3046715029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18142742661554213219">This is the only Canadian novel Canada I can think of might be called an epic. It may fall just short of that status and more closely fit the way an original review in Maclean's magazine described it: "a comic myth." The main flaw — noticeable in my rereading 30 years after initial publication — is Richler's tendency to drift from witty, incisive satire to clumsy and contemptuous sideswipes. The joke-filled saga of the devious and occasionally vicious Gursky clan revolves around a serious theme:</span>
  <span id="freeText18142742661554213219" style="display:none">This is the only Canadian novel Canada I can think of might be called an epic. It may fall just short of that status and more closely fit the way an original review in Maclean's magazine described it: "a comic myth." The main flaw — noticeable in my rereading 30 years after initial publication — is Richler's tendency to drift from witty, incisive satire to clumsy and contemptuous sideswipes. The joke-filled saga of the devious and occasionally vicious Gursky clan revolves around a serious theme: anti-Semitism; yes, even in Canada, and it's a little odd that many comments about the book do not mention the serious intent that Richler buried just below the surface of his broad humour. Solomon Gursky himself is a bit of a conundrum, a redeemer but also a Gursky through and through.<br />Rounded up from 4.5 stars on entertainment value.<br />Footnote 1: Richler would have finished writing the book by 1988 at the latest, but one of the characters expresses concern about reports of runaway global warming caused by carbon dioxide in the atmosphere. The relatively early mention probably owes something to Richler's habit of collecting interesting newspaper clippings.<br />Footnote 2: One of the character's descriptions of Canada is deliciously succinct sarcasm and remains entirely relevant in the political atmosphere of 2019: "Let me put it this way. Canada is not so much a country as a holding tank filled with the disgruntled progeny of defeated peoples."</span>
  <a data-text-id="18142742661554213219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1618769194" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1618769194" />
    <a title="Bill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4380068-bill"><img alt="Bill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600374592p2/4380068.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1618769194?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill" href="/user/show/4380068-bill">Bill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140277272" href="/book/show/978299.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=bill-s-favourites">bill-s-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=canlit">canlit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=top-ten-2016">top-ten-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1618769194" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10462406720288764421">What a joy to read! How do you describe <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/978299.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here' title='Solomon Gursky Was Here by Mordecai Richler' rel='nofollow noopener'>Solomon Gursky Was Here</a>? In its simplest form, it's the story of Ephraim Gursky, a minor crook and forger, who escapes from prison in England and tricks his way onto the ill-fated Franklin expedition, and manages to survive the disaster. He roams the Arctic, becomes a religious leader to a band of Eskimos; in some way he persuades them they are one of the 12 tribes of Israel. He eventually finds his way to Saskatchewan, starts a family and then the story f</span>
  <span id="freeText10462406720288764421" style="display:none">What a joy to read! How do you describe <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/978299.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here" title="Solomon Gursky Was Here by Mordecai Richler" rel="nofollow noopener">Solomon Gursky Was Here</a>? In its simplest form, it's the story of Ephraim Gursky, a minor crook and forger, who escapes from prison in England and tricks his way onto the ill-fated Franklin expedition, and manages to survive the disaster. He roams the Arctic, becomes a religious leader to a band of Eskimos; in some way he persuades them they are one of the 12 tribes of Israel. He eventually finds his way to Saskatchewan, starts a family and then the story follows his three grand-children; Solomon, Barnie and Morrie and their children. The grand-sons found successful liquor business, built partly on smuggling booze to the US during their prohibition; then settle in Montreal. The other aspect of the story follows one Moses Berger, son of poet LB Berger, who worked for the Gursky families. Moses goes through this story trying to find out the truth about Solomon Gursky; a trickster like his grand father, who died in a plane crash in the North of Canada. That is the story in its simplest form. It meanders from the past, following Ephraim, then his grand sons and their kids; also following Moses, now drunk, a failed writer as he explores the Gursky family. There are so many lovely tidbits, humor; just great, entertaining story-telling. It's an entertaining read and it winds up in such a satisfying manner; it was a pure joy to read. I highly recommend. It's been many years since I read something by Mordecai Richler and I'm going to have to find <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/766085.Barney_s_Version" title="Barney's Version by Mordecai Richler" rel="nofollow noopener">Barney's Version</a> next.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10462406720288764421" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1593573793" />
    <a title="Clementine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6100472-clementine"><img alt="Clementine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601233061p2/6100472.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1593573793?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clementine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clementine" href="/user/show/6100472-clementine">Clementine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6100472-clementine?shelf=canlit">canlit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6100472-clementine?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6100472-clementine?shelf=school">school</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6100472-clementine?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1593573793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9389776984933177718">This is my first foray into Mordecai Richler's adult fiction. I'm not sure why, but I wasn't expecting to enjoy it - perhaps because being assigned a nearly 600-page novel in the last few weeks of my degree is a bit of a bitter pill to swallow. However, I ended up actually quite enjoying it, mostly for the reason that people seem to dislike it: I'm a huge fan of multiple interlocking narratives, most especially if they aren't presented chronologically. Others have found the book difficult to fol</span>
  <span id="freeText9389776984933177718" style="display:none">This is my first foray into Mordecai Richler's adult fiction. I'm not sure why, but I wasn't expecting to enjoy it - perhaps because being assigned a nearly 600-page novel in the last few weeks of my degree is a bit of a bitter pill to swallow. However, I ended up actually quite enjoying it, mostly for the reason that people seem to dislike it: I'm a huge fan of multiple interlocking narratives, most especially if they aren't presented chronologically. Others have found the book difficult to follow, but I don't think it was too bad. There are a lot of characters to keep track of, especially in the Gursky family tree - it took me awhile to really get a grasp on how everyone was related to one another. But I don't generally have a hard time keeping track of complex, non-chronological narratives.<br /><br />I also loved the revisionist approach to Canadian history, another criticism of the book. I believe Margaret Atwood called it sacrilegious (blasphemous? something quite damning) to have Ephraim Gursky on the Franklin Expedition - but then, Atwood used the expedition very differently in her 1991 short story "The Age of Lead". Regardless, I thought it was like a delightful game of Where's Waldo? to spot Ephraim and Solomon all across history.<br /><br />I liked the writing style as well - acerbic, direct. Not that I'd expect any differently of a Montreal writer. I know I mentioned just the other day that I prefer texts with characters who I can relate to, but I thoroughly enjoyed <i>Solomon Gursky</i> despite the fact that there really isn't a single likeable character in the whole thing. I suppose a good, well-told tale can triumph for me once in awhile.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9389776984933177718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11531312989347298029" style="display:none">There were times when I believed "Solomon Gursky Was Here" would collapse under the weight of some many time lines, characters, and emotional baggage...it's the novel equivalent of the most exquisitely constructed Jenga tower...ready to topple over at any moment. Luckily it doesn't...and Mordechai Richler's most ambitious &amp; epic novel manages to hold together with stunning skill. This truly is an astonishing read, and shows a depth &amp; breadth of reach that many people might not have believed Mr. Richler capable of achieving. After reading "Gursky", throw any such doubts into the dustbin.<br /></span>

<div id="review_202649040" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202649040" />
    <a title="Karlo Mikhail" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6098273-karlo-mikhail"><img alt="Karlo Mikhail" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583230832p2/6098273.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202649040?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karlo Mikhail" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karlo Mikhail" href="/user/show/6098273-karlo-mikhail">Karlo Mikhail</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202649040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17806356217651989108">In what is touted by critics as possibly Canadian author Mordecai Richler’s best novel, we accompany Moses Berger in his obsessive quest to unravel the secrets of the Gurskys, a Jewish family who ran one of the biggest Canadian business empires. Berger, the son of a Jewish poet, a drunkard and an unsuccessful writer, particularly searches for traces of Solomon Gursky, the most enigmatic of the three Gursky brothers who saw the rise of their family’s fortune during the prohibition years as bootle</span>
  <span id="freeText17806356217651989108" style="display:none">In what is touted by critics as possibly Canadian author Mordecai Richler’s best novel, we accompany Moses Berger in his obsessive quest to unravel the secrets of the Gurskys, a Jewish family who ran one of the biggest Canadian business empires. Berger, the son of a Jewish poet, a drunkard and an unsuccessful writer, particularly searches for traces of Solomon Gursky, the most enigmatic of the three Gursky brothers who saw the rise of their family’s fortune during the prohibition years as bootleggers and rumrunners. Fleeing court action against him and his family, Solomon reportedly died in a plane crash.<br /><br />Thus, following Berger, we zigzag through time and place, from 19th century London, the Arctic Circle, up to 20th century Canada. There’s the adventure of the Gursky patriarch, Ephraim, a criminal from London who joins the ill-fated Franklin expedition to the Arctic, survives it and lives with Inuit tribes, and then swindles hapless pioneers in 19th century Canadian frontier lands. We read Ephraim’s grandchildren’s exploits in building their family empire, their dysfunctional lives, and the feuds that followed over their wealth. Berger’s own storyline, his childhood and the life of an unsuccessful writer in the figure of Berger’s own father.<br /><br />Newspaper clippings, letters, even a chapter from a novel supposedly written by one of the characters, and other memorabilia that adds to Berger’s investigation are inserted in between chapters. Historical figures hobnob with fictional ones. And since Berger’s quest (and thus the narrative) spans six generations of the Gurskys, we get a family tree (and a map of Canada) before the first chapter begins. It’s a wonder how each disparate scene are weaved together into a richly coherent whole.<br /><br /><b>
  <a href="https://karlomongaya.wordpress.com/2009/04/22/solomon-gursky-was-here/" rel="nofollow noopener">From Solomon Gursky Was Here</a>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="17806356217651989108" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_105044733" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="1.1" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3728503-1-1"><img alt="1.1" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307338551p2/3728503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/105044733?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="1.1" class="user" itemprop="url" name="1.1" href="/user/show/3728503-1-1">1.1</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140277272" href="/book/show/978299.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer105044733" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17773477037567233847">Can Lit is sort of in thrall to 'Arctic Narratives', and multi-generational epics seem to be a dime-a-dozen just about anywhere, so I was leery of this book at first. But I had read Richler before, and high hopes compelled me to press on despite being a bit confused at the start (I had to flip compulsively to the Gursky family tree every chapter). The narration is nonlinear, so there's your prerequisite dose of bewildering postmodernism I guess, but I found it well-paced and effective.<br/><br/>The dialog</span>
  <span id="freeText17773477037567233847" style="display:none">Can Lit is sort of in thrall to 'Arctic Narratives', and multi-generational epics seem to be a dime-a-dozen just about anywhere, so I was leery of this book at first. But I had read Richler before, and high hopes compelled me to press on despite being a bit confused at the start (I had to flip compulsively to the Gursky family tree every chapter). The narration is nonlinear, so there's your prerequisite dose of bewildering postmodernism I guess, but I found it well-paced and effective.<br /><br />The dialogue is consistently good, the characters are swell, the plot is coherent, and the patrilineal angst is in effect. Best of all, there are many poignant and funny moments throughout the novel, and the revelation of the character and history of Ephraim Gursky alone is worth many times the price of admittance. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17773477037567233847" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer377942141" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17686132829278846273">This is the story of the founding of a family dynasty through the eyes of an outside chronicler, Moses Berger, who is the son of a poet who the family supported. Really, the book is about many things: an obsession with those who are more financially fortunate, living in the shadow of our parents, Jewish experience throughout the world, the North and experiences of the North, family business and prohibition, and, I may be reaching here, who owns the past and who owns Canada's past. </span>

<div id="review_3245021511" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3245021511" />
    <a title="Josh Friedlander" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7226831-josh-friedlander"><img alt="Josh Friedlander" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498135749p2/7226831.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3245021511?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh Friedlander" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh Friedlander" href="/user/show/7226831-josh-friedlander">Josh Friedlander</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099877301" href="/book/show/862408.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7226831-josh-friedlander?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7226831-josh-friedlander?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7226831-josh-friedlander?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7226831-josh-friedlander?shelf=yiddish">yiddish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3245021511" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14894261994547784126">I've read my fair share of books by Canadians - this one makes it three of my last four! - but above any of them, this book seemed <i>essentially</i> Canadian, historical fiction steeped in Canada's unique aspects: its polar wastes and Franco-English divide, its closer relationship with Britain, its small-country feel within neverending borders. This long, twisting, family saga of a Prohibition-era booze fortune - somewhat paralleling the real-life <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronfman_family' rel='nofollow noopener'>Bronfman family</a> and Seagram - is told out of order in </span>
  <span id="freeText14894261994547784126" style="display:none">I've read my fair share of books by Canadians - this one makes it three of my last four! - but above any of them, this book seemed <i>essentially</i> Canadian, historical fiction steeped in Canada's unique aspects: its polar wastes and Franco-English divide, its closer relationship with Britain, its small-country feel within neverending borders. This long, twisting, family saga of a Prohibition-era booze fortune - somewhat paralleling the real-life <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronfman_family" rel="nofollow noopener">Bronfman family</a> and Seagram - is told out of order in fits and starts, somewhat about Moshe Berger, an aspirational author surrogate (Rhodes scholar historian and freelance writer, hobnobbing with aristocrats while procrastinating on his graduate thesis on Beveridge), who has an affair with a Gursky scion and falls into chronicling the family. The woman in question is something of a ditz - the women in this novel are either airheaded bombshells or shrews; the male characters simply blackguards. There is no-one to like - more on this later.<br /><br />Maybe a good way to think about Richler’s goal - besides cocking a snook at snobby anti-Semites of both the Anglo- and Francophone variety, eventually in a scene which (no spoilers!) I found distasteful in multiple senses - is to write a paean to family historians. Seeing characters come to life, from bearded sepia figures in overcoats to living human beings you might have known, is a thrill, something I would love to experience with my own family pictures. And as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61145960" rel="nofollow noopener">another reader on here has pointed out</a>, Richler really knows his Jewish stuff, unlike Michael Chabon whose <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16703.The_Yiddish_Policemen_s_Union" title="The Yiddish Policemen's Union by Michael Chabon" rel="nofollow noopener">The Yiddish Policemen Union</a> has surely drawn inspiration from his shtetl Jews in the land of the midnight sun (in one gag, new Jewish converts are told they must fast from sundown to sundown on Yom Kippur, leading to mass starvation since this amounts to several months). He’s similarly done his reading on the Franklin Expedition and the Victorian underworld. But why not read a true version - say, Hadley Freeman’s recent and excellent-sounding <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49879670.House_of_Glass_The_Story_and_Secrets_of_a_Twentieth_Century_Jewish_Family" title="House of Glass The Story and Secrets of a Twentieth-Century Jewish Family by Hadley Freeman" rel="nofollow noopener">House of Glass: The Story and Secrets of a Twentieth-Century Jewish Family</a> - than this zany, sagging satire?<br /><br />Because to reiterate, this book is l-o-n-g. It’s boring to complain about "unlikable characters", but after hundreds of pages you really start to get tired of everyone in this book, from the feckless scoundrel founding father Ephraim to Isaac, the youngest of the spoiled-rotten descendants. Did we really need so much of this? And jokes which may have seemed edgy and sharp in the late 80s now sound tone-deaf: racist (writing a Chinese-Canadian man's speech in dialect, Ephraim "predicting" an eclipse to awe the ignorant Inuit savages), homophobic and sexist. Do I sound preachy? I would feel more accommodating if this book had been, say, FOUR HUNDRED PAGES SHORTER.<br /><br />Lastly: two instances of the <a href="https://www.reference.com/world-view/baader-meinhof-phenomenon-b5261502b8a61dac" rel="nofollow noopener">Baader–Meinhof phenomenon</a> - one of the characters in this sprawling novel (a half-Inuit Lubavitcher <i>hasid</i>!) is a believer in the global cooling thing I talked about in relation to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/636223.Ice" title="Ice by Anna Kavan" rel="nofollow noopener">Ice</a>… The other is the kingdom of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prester_John" rel="nofollow noopener">Prester John</a>, which I first encountered last month in Quine’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/469713.Pursuit_of_Truth" title="Pursuit of Truth by Willard Van Orman Quine" rel="nofollow noopener">Pursuit of Truth</a>. (Is there a symbolism in the Christian search for a mystical utopia vs Jewish pragmatism? Eh, wouldn’t bet on it.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14894261994547784126" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3127534316" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3127534316" />
    <a title="Julia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26589359-julia"><img alt="Julia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594266241p2/26589359.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3127534316?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julia" href="/user/show/26589359-julia">Julia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143051459" href="/book/show/907803.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26589359-julia?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3127534316&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3127534316" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1606936770611716888">indeed, he was!<br/><br/>embroiling jewry into every major canadian historical event of the past 200 years (not to mention the international ones!) is pure chutzpah. it's this quality that makes mordecai richler so riotous and touching to me - in everything he wrote, he goes all in. the gurskys are larger than life, yet even in the final pages richler draws them back to montreal - to the family mansion in westmount, no less. <br/><br/>in the second grade, i was assigned to present to the class on any canadian provi</span>
  <span id="freeText1606936770611716888" style="display:none">indeed, he was!<br /><br />embroiling jewry into every major canadian historical event of the past 200 years (not to mention the international ones!) is pure chutzpah. it's this quality that makes mordecai richler so riotous and touching to me - in everything he wrote, he goes all in. the gurskys are larger than life, yet even in the final pages richler draws them back to montreal - to the family mansion in westmount, no less. <br /><br />in the second grade, i was assigned to present to the class on any canadian province of my choosing. my dad is originally from montreal - my family left for toronto in the early 70s - so of course i had to pick quebec. for more than half my time in front of the class, all i could do was go on about mordecai richler. the jacob two-two tv show had recently taken over my life, and at home i would look up to find my dad's copy of barney's version on his bookshelf, listening as he would go on about how good the film adaptation of duddy kravitz was to me. <br /><br />being jewish in toronto can at times feel like being a montreal jew, twice removed. the city looms large in the collective memory, both of my family and of canada itself - listening to stories of my dad growing up in snowdon, going to the cinema and the deli beside it, feels as definitive to me as reading richler's characters trudge up and down st. urbain. you know the exact spots to go, which bakeries have the best bagels, who attended which public school, and all the gossip of mcgill alumni, even if they are six hours away. the city has been idolized as a key originating point for so long that it feels intrinsic to canadian jewish identity, and seeing richler give the same treatment to the rest of the country and it's tales is an absolute delight. <br /><br />gursky spans generations, and the points of genealogy can be hazy at best. but instead of seeming lackadaisical, it feels true to life. moses berger's unrelenting research to fill in the gaps of such an infuriating family is compounded by complications even further than the circumstances - rampant alcoholism, mysterious disappearances, and relationships so fragmented it's a wonder the whole mctavish enterprise can stay afloat. it's well deserving of its 540 pages - for all its cyclicity, revisionist history, and undeniable personality. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1606936770611716888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2828089923" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2828089923" />
    <a title="Jerry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21430140-jerry"><img alt="Jerry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566521416p2/21430140.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2828089923?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jerry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jerry" href="/user/show/21430140-jerry">Jerry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394539959" href="/book/show/1775541.Solomon_Gursky_Was_Here">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2828089923" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14773061536586344654"><blockquote><br/>	There was, they said, ice lodged in Mr. Bernard’s heart, glacial ice, but he had come by it honestly.<br/></blockquote><br/><br/>Moses Berger is the failed son of a famous, in the right circles, poet; this may be why he obsesses over the history of the Gursky family with its many failed sons. The book jumps back and forth between people and ages; from Ephraim Gursky, who came from London to the great white north, through his three grandsons (his son Aaron gets very little attention) and great grandsons and granddaughters.</span>
  <span id="freeText14773061536586344654" style="display:none"><blockquote><br />	There was, they said, ice lodged in Mr. Bernard’s heart, glacial ice, but he had come by it honestly.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />Moses Berger is the failed son of a famous, in the right circles, poet; this may be why he obsesses over the history of the Gursky family with its many failed sons. The book jumps back and forth between people and ages; from Ephraim Gursky, who came from London to the great white north, through his three grandsons (his son Aaron gets very little attention) and great grandsons and granddaughters. It jumps from the 1800s (at least 1851) and into the eighties (at least 1983). The paranoids in the book (and there is a lot of paranoia, some overt, some subtle) go from a fear of a new ice age to a fear of frying.<br /><br />It jumps from Washington DC to the Polar Sea and, in some asides, far south.<br /><br />It is told mostly in the order in which Moses discovers a topic, which makes it nearly impossible to follow; there is a family tree in the front to help orient the reader. It lacks years, because the reader needs to be disoriented. Everybody else is.<br /><br />Most of the attention goes to Moses, who is financially and sexually tied up with the Gurskys; and Solomon Gursky, who learned wilderness skills, and potentially quite a few other skills, from Ephraim, and who is, literally, everywhere.<br /><br />It is filled with the stories of unreliable narrators as told by unreliable narrators. Some of the stories seem nearly supernatural, drawing from both “Eskimaux” and Jewish traditions.<br /><br />The generations repeat the mistakes of their ancestors, making later stories seem weirdly familiar. They also repeat rituals, deliberately and perhaps accidentally, in keeping with the uncertain supernatural elements.<br /><br />I was inspired to read this book by a combination of (1) preparing for a Montreal visit last year, and, when looking for dead authors to stalk, discovering the name Mordecai Richler, and (2) reading Mark Steyn’s essay on Richler in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/200168.From_Head_to_Toe_An_Anatomical_Anthology" title="From Head to Toe An Anatomical Anthology by Mark Steyn" rel="nofollow noopener">From Head to Toe</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14773061536586344654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63821805-hotaru?shelf=canadian-author">canadian-author</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2711818631" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13425633550949474718">What a hard book to rate ! Mordecai Richler doesn't pull any punches and is not kind his readers, especially if the latest have just a vague knowledge of Judaism and Jewish culture. A lot of information is given, many allusions are made that you'll probably understand only if you read the book 2 or 3 times, PoVs mercilessly jump from one character to the next, from one CENTURY to the next. One of the main characters, a kind scholar/drunkard obsessed with the rich and shady Gursky family, is not </span>
  <span id="freeText13425633550949474718" style="display:none">What a hard book to rate ! Mordecai Richler doesn't pull any punches and is not kind his readers, especially if the latest have just a vague knowledge of Judaism and Jewish culture. A lot of information is given, many allusions are made that you'll probably understand only if you read the book 2 or 3 times, PoVs mercilessly jump from one character to the next, from one CENTURY to the next. One of the main characters, a kind scholar/drunkard obsessed with the rich and shady Gursky family, is not very likeable, but then there are no shining heroes in this story. But in the end, you can't help being pulled in the whirlwind that are the Three Gursky Brothers and their grandfather Ephraïm. Some truly striking nuggets of writings about Canada can be also found in the book, which I would definitely recommend. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13425633550949474718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText8096419284717705685" style="display:none">I enjoyed this book a lot. The way the story was told took a bit to get into, and some of the time jumping was jarring at times, but the Gursky family's intrigue was fascinating. I did not love the ending, as I felt it was somewhat hurriedly wrapped up. It was also not completely definitive what had happened which left me somewhat unsatisfied. But the rest was great. I do wish Morrie's character had been developed a bit more on the page instead of realizing there was so much more to him after the fact.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3312975279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15819058070814499950">This was a re-read and I was interested by the fact that Richler mentions the Franklin Expedition as the start of the Gursky family entering the periphery of Canadian life and then becoming a major player. <br/>Solomon Gursky is the elusive figure in all of this and despite being unpleasant, had charm unlike his other two brothers.<br/>Sadly, this book is very dated and occasionally vulgar with no real reason to be. Again, a portrait of a Canada long gone.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2104492890" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2104492890" />
    <a title="Vzenari" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32466012-vzenari"><img alt="Vzenari" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403554963p2/32466012.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2104492890?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vzenari" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vzenari" href="/user/show/32466012-vzenari">Vzenari</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2104492890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14819313246843311978">I have tried Mordecai Richler and failed. He can be quite funny, yes, and I like the idea of a Jewish trickster. I don’t have the patience, though, for the machismo and the repetitive details around the crotchety Magog community and the garrulous, backstabbing Gurskys. Since I don’t share Richler’s obsessions with wealth, big boobs and knockdowns of Quebecois culture, I found the book a slog.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2515183760" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2515183760" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31818441-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2515183760?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/31818441-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31818441-tony?shelf=personal">personal</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2515183760&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2515183760" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8745114570145637382">Convoluted but intriguing family saga of Jewish folk in the far north. Ephrain Gursky arrives with the Inuit as a product of the search for the Northwest Passage. The story freewheels through cannibalism, religious beliefs, alcohol running during the prohibition and the search of Moses Berger for a father to replace his own (who found his son to be too much of a threat).</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sTPjsSRq8mWLtp7Ko/C2roZQr9a3OMRRMt1/yaaKcaj0KNhZcxCY5ru7t4NZ8cJ7J5V+TuTB+43LB+H9XsvUmg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Stubborn, querulous, self-reliant – and, at ninety, with her life nearly behind her – Hagar Shipley makes <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3483080909913248060\" style=\"display:none\">In her best-loved novel, The Stone Angel, Margaret Laurence introduces Hagar Shipley, one of the most memorable characters in Canadian fiction. Stubborn, querulous, self-reliant – and, at ninety, with her life nearly behind her – Hagar Shipley makes a bold last step towards freedom and independence.As her story unfolds, we are drawn into her past. We meet Hagar as a young girl growing up in a black prairie town; as the wife of a virile but unsuccessful farmer with whom her marriage was stormy; as a mother who dominates her younger son; and, finally, as an old woman isolated by an uncompromising pride and by the stern virtues she has inherited from her pioneer ancestors.Vivid, evocative, moving, The Stone Angel celebrates the triumph of the spirit, and reveals Margaret Laurence at the height of her powers as a writer of extraordinary craft and profound insight into the workings of the human heart.From the Hardcover edition.<\/span>\n All of the firs<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2437373516460400508\" style=\"display:none\">Drood… is the name and nightmare that obsesses Charles Dickens for the last five years of his life.\n\nOn June 9, 1865, Dickens and his mistress are secretly returning to London, when their express train hurtles over a gap in a trestle. All of the first-class carriages except the one carrying Dickens are smashed to bits in the valley below. When Dickens descends into that valley to confront the dead and dying, his life will be changed forever. And at the core of that ensuing five-year nightmare is…\n\nDrood… the name that Dickens whispers to his friend Wilkie Collins. A laudanum addict and lesser novelist, Collins flouts Victorian sensibilities by living with one mistress while having a child with another, but he may be the only man on Earth with whom Dickens can share the secret of…\n\nDrood. Increasingly obsessed with crypts, cemeteries, and the precise length of time it would take for a corpse to dissolve in a lime pit, Dickens ceases writing for four years and wanders the worst slums and catacombs of London at night while staging public readings during the day, gruesome readings that leave his audiences horrified. Finally he begins writing what would have been the world’s first great mystery masterpiece, The Mystery of Edwin Drood, only to be interrupted forever by…\n\nDrood.\n\nBased on actual biographical events, Drood explores the still-unresolved mysteries of one of our greatest writer’s dark final days in a profoundly original tale that confirms Lincoln Child’s assessment of New York Times bestselling author Dan Simmons as “a giant among novelists.”<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2437373516460400508\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/114539.Mordecai_Richler">About Mordecai Richler</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/114539.Mordecai_Richler"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1227583751p3/114539.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/114539.Mordecai_Richler">Mordecai Richler
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
262
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11910281085816825715">Mordecai Richler was a Canadian author, screenwriter and essayist. <br/><br/>His best known works are The Apprenticeship of Duddy Kravitz (1959) and Barney's Version (1997); his 1989 novel Solomon Gursky Was Here was shortlisted for the Man Booker Prize in 1990. He was also well known for the Jacob Two-Two children's stories. .<br/><br/>The son of a Jewish scrap yard dealer, Richler was born in 1931 and raised on St.</span>
  <span id="freeText11910281085816825715" style="display:none">Mordecai Richler was a Canadian author, screenwriter and essayist. <br /><br />His best known works are The Apprenticeship of Duddy Kravitz (1959) and Barney's Version (1997); his 1989 novel Solomon Gursky Was Here was shortlisted for the Man Booker Prize in 1990. He was also well known for the Jacob Two-Two children's stories. .<br /><br />The son of a Jewish scrap yard dealer, Richler was born in 1931 and raised on St. Urbain Street in the Mile End area of Montreal. He learned Yiddish and English, and graduated from Baron Byng High School. Richler enrolled in Sir George Williams College (now Concordia University) to study English but dropped out before completing his degree. <br /><br />Years later, Leah Rosenberg, Richler's mother, published an autobiography, The Errand Runner: Memoirs of a Rabbi's Daughter (1981), which discusses Mordecai's birth and upbringing, and the sometime difficult relationship between them.<br /><br />Richler moved to Paris at age nineteen, intent on following in the footsteps of a previous generation of literary exiles, the so-called Lost Generation of the 1920s, many of whom were from the United States.<br /><br />Richler returned to Montreal in 1952, working briefly at the Canadian Broadcasting Corporation, then moved to London in 1954. He published seven of his ten novels while living in London, as well as considerable journalism.<br /><br />Worrying "about being so long away from the roots of my discontent", Richler returned to Montreal in 1972. He wrote repeatedly about the Jewish community of Montreal and especially about his former neighborhood, portraying it in multiple novels.<br /><br />In England, in 1954, Richler married Catherine Boudreau, a French-Canadian divorcee nine years his senior. On the eve of their wedding, he met and was smitten by Florence Mann (née Wood), a young married woman.<br /><br />Some years later Richler and Mann both divorced and married each other. He adopted her son Daniel. The couple had five children together: Daniel, Jacob, Noah, Martha and Emma. These events inspired his novel Barney's Version.<br /><br />Richler died of cancer at the age of 70.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11910281085816825715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
